<div class="section" id="sec4"><div class="header article-hdr"><div class="kicker">		                        SECTION IV.</div><h2>Demonstration Prototype</h2></div><p>In this section, a functional demonstration prototype of the battery-free system is described, including the developed RFID RC device and the DeC (in this case, a TV). As a proof of concept, the RC system is integrated in a TV equipment and some control functionalities are implemented and tested. To do so, the four-key RC prototype of <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig8" class="fulltext-link">Fig. 8(c)</a> is used to implement channel up (CH+), channel down (CH−), volume up (Vol+) and volume down (Vol−) functionalities.</p><p>Concerning the communication between the proposed RFID RC and the DeC, two options exist as exemplified in <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig1" class="fulltext-link">Figs. 1(a)</a> and <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig9" class="fulltext-link">9(a)</a>: the first case requires that an RFID reader front-end is embedded in the DeC during the manufacturing process. Such an RFID front-end consists of a transmitter, which sends data (RFID commands) and wireless power to the RC device, and a receiver responsible for receiving the backscattered data from the RC. In this case, both the RFID transmitter and receiver are embedded in the DeC and a common control unit can be used. Alternatively, as illustrated in <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig9" class="fulltext-link">Fig. 9(a)</a>, an interface/adaptor unit can be externally mounted in the DeC. Such interface unit comprises an RFID reader and antennas and an RFID-to-IR converter that acts as a bridge between the RFID reader and the DeC. In this case, the RFID reader has an independent control unit. In this scenario, there is no direct electrical contact between the RFID reader and DeC. Instead, the communication between the RC and the DeC is made in two steps: the RC communicates with the interface unit by RF backscattering (RFID) and the communication between the interface unit and the DeC is implemented using IR. This is especially useful to incorporate the proposed scheme into an already installed system that uses conventional IR technology (e.g., an old TV).</p><p>
</p><div class="figure figure-full" id="fig9"><!--          Workaround for combined images.Eg.- 1000116 Fig. 5        --><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/6550039/6522276/6522276-fig-9-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/6550039/6522276/6522276-fig-9-small.gif" alt="Figure 9" /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">Fig. 9. </b><fig><p>(a) Diagram of the system using an external RFID-IR adapter to interact with the TV. (b) Demonstration prototype: 1—RFID reader, 2—reader antennas, 3—external RFID-IR interface and universal IR RC. In a final industrial implementation, all the external hardware (RFID reader and antennas) would be miniaturized and embedded in the TV. The control software, now running in a computer, would be implemented in the TV control unit.</p></fig></div><p class="links"><a href="/document/6522276/all-figures" class="all">View All</a></p></div>
<div class="figure figure-full" id="fig10"><!--          Workaround for combined images.Eg.- 1000116 Fig. 5        --><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/6550039/6522276/6522276-fig-10-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/6550039/6522276/6522276-fig-10-small.gif" alt="Figure 10" /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">Fig. 10. </b><fig><p>RFID–IR interface including a universal IR RC. In this prototype, it acts as a bridge between the RFID reader and the TV: it receives information from the reader via a digital I/O interface and it sends this information to the TV via IR signals.</p></fig></div><p class="links"><a href="/document/6522276/all-figures" class="all">View All</a></p></div>
Since the first option—RFID reader embedded in the DeC—requires more time to be implemented and because we are only interested in providing a simple proof of concept, in this work the second option is followed. To do so, a commercial RFID reader kit <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref15" id="context_ref_15_4">[15]</a>, <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref16" id="context_ref_16_4">[16]</a> is combined with an universal IR RC to implement the above-mentioned RFID-IR interface (<a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig10" class="fulltext-link">Fig. 10</a>). The RFID reader kit can be controlled by a computer allowing the implementation custom applications. The used RFID kit provides a set of commands <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref16" id="context_ref_16_4">[16]</a>, namely to perform inventory of tags in the field, to read tags' ID, and to access tags' memory. Such commands are accessible through TCP-IP interface by using a custom software application.<a ref-type="fn" anchor="fn4" class="footnote-link"><sup>4</sup></a> The RFID reader also provides digital I/O interfaces equally controlled by the software application. These I/O interfaces are used to interface with the universal IR RC. The use of a universal RC for the demo prototype is quite useful since it allows the prototype to be adaptable to any equipment covered by the universal RC.<p></p><p>
</p><div class="figure figure-full" id="fig11"><!--          Workaround for combined images.Eg.- 1000116 Fig. 5        --><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/6550039/6522276/6522276-fig-11-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/6550039/6522276/6522276-fig-11-small.gif" alt="Figure 11" /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">Fig. 11. </b><fig><p>Demo application software that controls the RFID reader. In this case, the channel up icon is red (in online version) indicating that the corresponding key is being pressed in the RC.</p></fig></div><p class="links"><a href="/document/6522276/all-figures" class="all">View All</a></p></div>
<div class="figure figure-full" id="fig12"><!--          Workaround for combined images.Eg.- 1000116 Fig. 5        --><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/6550039/6522276/6522276-fig-12-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/6550039/6522276/6522276-fig-12-small.gif" alt="Figure 12" /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">Fig. 12. </b><fig><p>Simplified flowchart of the developed JAVA application software.</p></fig></div><p class="links"><a href="/document/6522276/all-figures" class="all">View All</a></p></div>
In <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig11" class="fulltext-link">Fig. 11</a>, the developed software interface is depicted. The application allows the configuration of the RFID reader (e.g., set output power level, start and stop scanning the field) and the display of the tags' ID. Information of the control actions being performed are is also displayed (CH−,CH+,VOL−, VOL+). The flowchart of the developed demo application is shown in <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig12" class="fulltext-link">Fig. 12</a>: the application sets the RFID reader to be continuously scanning its field searching for RFID tags. Once a tag is read, the application determines whether the read ID is valid or not. If it is a valid key ID, then the application activates the corresponding key in the universal IR RC through the reader I/O interface. The universal IR RC is placed in the proximity of the equipment to be controlled, allowing line-of-sight IR communication between the RFID–IR interface and the TV.<p></p><p><a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig9" class="fulltext-link">Fig. 9(b)</a> shows the complete transmitter/receiver setup including a TV, a commercial RFID reader and antennas, a computer running the demo application software, and a universal IR RC. Details of the RFID–IR interface are depicted in <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig10" class="fulltext-link">Fig. 10</a>.</p></div>
<div class="section" id="sec5"><div class="header article-hdr"><div class="kicker">		                        SECTION V.</div><h2>Conclusion and Future Work</h2></div><p>A batteryless RC system has been proposed based on a multi-RFID scheme. A multi-port microstrip network has been fabricated and measured and preliminary evaluations show the possibility of extending the concept to a large number of keys. A complete prototype of the battery-free RC unit and system has been implemented and tested. The system has been integrated in a TV, and as a proof of concept, four basic control functionalities have been successfully implemented: CH−,CH+,VOL−, and VOL+. Although the prototype is being tested with a TV equipment, this RC scheme can be applied to a variety of other RC systems, especially in scenarios where the use of batteries is undesirable. The proposed multi-RFID scheme can also find application in batteryless keyboards, multi-functionality cards, or user-controlled cards.</p><p>The proposed approach is based on a commercially available low-cost RFID technology compliant with <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref17" id="context_ref_17_5">[17]</a> and the RC unit can be manufactured at a very low cost, which makes the solution quite affordable and cost effective. The main drawback of the proposed approach is related to the need of having the RFID reader always turned on. Thus, further work is needed at the DeC side in order to optimize its energy efficiency. Future work will also focus on the improvement of the RC, concerning size reduction, increasing of number of keys, and antenna optimization.</p></div>
<h3>ACKNOWLEDGMENT</h3><p>The authors acknowledge R. Fernandes, University of Aveiro, Aveiro, Portugal, and H. Mostardinha and P. Gonçalves, both with the Institute of Telecommunications, University of Aveiro.</p></div></div>

<script>

function getCookie(name) {
	var nameEQ = name + "=";
	var ca = document.cookie.split(';');
	for(var i=0;i &lt; ca.length;i++) {
		var c = ca[i];
		while (c.charAt(0)==' ') c = c.substring(1,c.length);
		if (c.indexOf(nameEQ) == 0) return c.substring(nameEQ.length,c.length);
	}
	return null;
}

	if(getCookie('legacyUserName')!=null)
	{
		if(IBP_WS_ENABLED_FLAG){
		Modal.refreshLegacyAccountTransition('/xpl/mwLegacyAccountTransition.jsp');
		}
	}
});

</script>

The proposed RC device does not require the use of batteries or other installed power source. Instead, it relies on passive RFID chips that are remotely powered by an RFID reader. The controlled device (e.g., a TV) incorporates an RFID reader to power up and communicate with the RC. The proposed batteryless RC is composed of an antenna, a plurality of &lt;i&gt;N&lt;/i&gt; passive RFID chips and &lt;i&gt;N&lt;/i&gt; switches, and a multi-port microstrip network that interconnects the various RFID chips, allowing them to share a common antenna. Each key of the RC is associated to an RFID with a unique identifier, which allows the device to be controlled to identify the key pressed by the user. The proposed arrangement ensures that only the chip associated to the pressed key is read by the RFID reader, while the other chips remain inactive. First of all, the system is described including the multi-RFID scheme and the proposed multi-port network. Afterword, a characterization of the contact switches and RFID chips is performed. This is followed by RFID chip impedance matching and switch tuning. A multi-port network is fabricated (in low-cost FR4) and measured in order to access network's behavior depending on some parameters such as the number of ports and distance from the active port to the antenna. Finally, a four-key RC unit is prototyped, and an RFID reader system is integrated in a TV by using an external RFID-to-infrared interface. Four control functionalities are implemented and tested (CH+, CH-, Vol+, and Vol-). Measurements are also conducted to evaluate the system coverage range and line-of-sight
